  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_596c2960-212b-44e8-aba3-88c93d8ef27a'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer16874262487188448169"><b>The Prince of the Forest</b> <br/> Bambi's life in the woods begins happily. There are forest animals to play with -- Friend Hare, the chattery squirrel, the noisy screech owl, and Bambi's twin cousins, frail Gobo and beautiful Faline. <br/> But winter comes, and Bambi learns that the woods hold danger -- and things he doesn't understand.   <span id="freeText16874262487188448169" style="display:none"><b>The Prince of the Forest</b> <br /> Bambi's life in the woods begins happily. There are forest animals to play with -- Friend Hare, the chattery squirrel, the noisy screech owl, and Bambi's twin cousins, frail Gobo and beautiful Faline. <br /> But winter comes, and Bambi learns that the woods hold danger -- and things he doesn't understand. The first snowfall makes food hard to find. Bambi's father, a handsome stag, roams the forest, but leaves Bambi and his mother alone. <br /> Then there is Man. He comes to the forest with weapons that can wound an animal. He does terrible things to Gobo, to Bambi's mother, and even to Bambi. But He can't keep Bambi from growing into a handsome stag himself, and becoming...the Prince of the Forest.</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1108365-do-you-know-what-are-the-differences">Do you know what are the differences between this book and the Disney animated movie?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1108365'><a id="like_count_community_question_1108365" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1108365?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">3 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F739840.Bambi">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/1108365-do-you-know-what-are-the-differences">3 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1108365-do-you-know-what-are-the-differences">See all 5 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/33511731-clint"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1588562177i/33511731._UY100_CR6,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/33511731-clint">Clint</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    Thumper and Flower are not in the book; they were created for the movie. In the book, Faline has a brother named Gobo, who was not in the movie. At on<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    Thumper and Flower are not in the book; they were created for the movie. In the book, Faline has a brother named Gobo, who was not in the movie. At one point Gobo gets taken away by humans and kept as a pet, then returns to the forest later on and tells the other animals what humans are like. There are other deer in the book as well, that are all mentioned by name.<br /><br />The movie really cranks up the cuteness factor, and is really watered down in comparison to the book, to be honest. The animals act more like, well, animals in the book...there's no skating around on ice, no getting "twitterpated" or anything like that. There's still dialogue, though, the animals do talk in the book. What they talk about is more in line with how animals actually think and what they experience. It's a lot more realistic overall. Not all the animals get along, some of them kill each other for food, and there are mentions of blood at times. The book paints a more accurate mental picture of nature in that regard.<br /><br />The book is kinda deep, too. A lot of it can be taken as a metaphor for life. Like the changing of the seasons for example, from the warm summer to the cool autumn to the harsh winter, can be seen as a metaphor for the changes that we all go through in our lives, from good times to bad times, then back to good again, and so on. (The chapter about the two leaves is particularly brilliant in that regard, in a simple yet powerful way.) Also, at one point Bambi comes to the realization that Man is not all-powerful and is ultimately subject to the same laws of nature that the rest of the animals are.<br /><br />One good thing I can say about the movie--other than the beautiful animation and music, that is--is that Man is never shown onscreen at any point. The animals are warned of his approach by screeching birds in the distance. This happens in the book as well, and of course the deer can also smell humans from far away, but humans are actually visible to the deer a few times in the book. In the movie however, Man is always an unseen threat, and this was a deliberate choice made my Walt Disney and his crew, who realized that an unknown danger is scarier than something that you actually get to see, especially in a film.<br /><br />I would definitely recommend reading the book. It's a pretty easy read.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>
</div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1479146609" />
    <a title="Justin Tate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23082940-justin-tate"><img alt="Justin Tate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593157920p2/23082940.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1479146609?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Justin Tate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Justin Tate" href="/user/show/23082940-justin-tate">Justin Tate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1479146609" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10744429699133629580">Bambi is one of Disney's darkest films, but it's lighthearted whimsy compared to the original novel which would require trigger warnings nowadays. Thumper is brutally slaughtered as gunshots reverberate throughout the meadow and pools of blood stain the daisies. Death is everywhere--even in the trees. One of the saddest moments occurs when two lone oak leaves ponder the afterlife before tumbling to their demise from bare branches.<br/><br/>It's gritty and heartbreaking, yet also heartlifting. Exceedingly </span>
  <span id="freeText10744429699133629580" style="display:none">Bambi is one of Disney's darkest films, but it's lighthearted whimsy compared to the original novel which would require trigger warnings nowadays. Thumper is brutally slaughtered as gunshots reverberate throughout the meadow and pools of blood stain the daisies. Death is everywhere--even in the trees. One of the saddest moments occurs when two lone oak leaves ponder the afterlife before tumbling to their demise from bare branches.<br /><br />It's gritty and heartbreaking, yet also heartlifting. Exceedingly profound. One of the few novels that's lingered with me for nearly a decade. No one describes nature better than Felix Salten, and certainly no other animal-perspective novel can compare. I'm reading Watership Down currently and, though it has many strengths, it's making me nostalgic for this novel--a true masterpiece. Bambi is one of those books, tight and concise, which you can read over and over and always learn something new.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10744429699133629580" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1981776041" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1981776041" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7365405-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522094190p2/7365405.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1981776041?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/7365405-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7365405-james?shelf=2-fic-childrens">2-fic-childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7365405-james?shelf=1-fiction">1-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1981776041" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3274235155292656258"><b>
  <i>
    <u>Book Review</u>
  </i>
</b><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/739840.Bambi' title='Bambi by Felix Salten' rel='nofollow noopener'>Bambi</a>, a children's / young adult book published in 1923 by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/262262.Felix_Salten' title='Felix Salten' rel='nofollow noopener'>Felix Salten</a>, is very different than the one we've seen from Walt Disney. Many have seen the cartoon versions, felt sad for the death and know it as a classic "growing-up" book for kids. But it's really a whole lot more, if you choose to read the original version. And for that, it gets 4 of 5 stars for this reader. The book is not written as a children's story, i.e. for a 4 or 5 year old to look at pictures, read </span>
  <span id="freeText3274235155292656258" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>
    <u>Book Review</u>
  </i>
</b><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/739840.Bambi" title="Bambi by Felix Salten" rel="nofollow noopener">Bambi</a>, a children's / young adult book published in 1923 by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/262262.Felix_Salten" title="Felix Salten" rel="nofollow noopener">Felix Salten</a>, is very different than the one we've seen from Walt Disney. Many have seen the cartoon versions, felt sad for the death and know it as a classic "growing-up" book for kids. But it's really a whole lot more, if you choose to read the original version. And for that, it gets 4 of 5 stars for this reader. The book is not written as a children's story, i.e. for a 4 or 5 year old to look at pictures, read the funny captions and laugh at the adventure of the animals. It's a true coming-of-age story for a deer, told with all the ramifications of life's expectations that eventually occur. Bambi has a beautiful relationship with his parents and family. He watches his friends play games and grow older. He sees courtship and love. He learns what it means to be afraid. He sees death. He learns to be a protector. It's a full picture story, meant to show the realities of animals living in the forest. It's a stronger story than I'd watched in the Disney films, but then again, I waited to read it until I was almost 13 years old.<br /><br />It's a definite must-read for kids, but probably not alone until they are at least 10. I don't even think it's a good one to read to your kids much earlier than that... you may want them to see the cartoon version first, as I think that has a lot of emotion packed into it... but I see the other side of the coin, where it's important to teach reality to them, even at a young age.<br /><br /><b>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2023867444" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2023867444" />
    <a title="Bionic Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean"><img alt="Bionic Jean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586709362p2/19300421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2023867444?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bionic Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bionic Jean" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean">Bionic Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/35391022-bambi">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=animals">animals</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=read-authors-q-t">read-authors-q-t</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=caravan">caravan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=antiquarian-books">antiquarian-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2023867444" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1998947785675594894">How come I have never read this little treasure before now? <br/><br/><b>Bambi: A life in the Woods</b> is a true classic of children’s literature, with passages of great joy and also of deep regret and sorrow; exhilarating, but also occasionally savage, and heavy with exquisite prose descriptions of the woods and countryside. The writer was evidently a keen naturalist, with an eye for details of flora and fauna throughout the seasons, and also skilled at his craft. His name was Felix Salten.<br/><br/>Felix Salten was an </span>
  <span id="freeText1998947785675594894" style="display:none">How come I have never read this little treasure before now? <br /><br /><b>Bambi: A life in the Woods</b> is a true classic of children’s literature, with passages of great joy and also of deep regret and sorrow; exhilarating, but also occasionally savage, and heavy with exquisite prose descriptions of the woods and countryside. The writer was evidently a keen naturalist, with an eye for details of flora and fauna throughout the seasons, and also skilled at his craft. His name was Felix Salten.<br /><br />Felix Salten was an Austrian writer, born in Hungary in 1869. He was a prolific writer of short stories and novels, often about animals, travel books, and essay collections. He was also an Art and literary critic, and wrote articles for nearly all the major newspapers of Vienna. But what he is most famous for is his outstanding and memorable novel for children, <b>Bambi, a Life in the Woods</b>, which was first published in German as <i>“Bambi: Eine Lebensgeschichte aus dem Walde”</i> in 1923, and translated into English in 1928.<br /><br />In 1942, Walt Disney made the classic animated film <i>“Bambi”</i>. But if your only experience of Bambi so far has been courtesy of Walt Disney, you will find reading this a much broader and deeper experience. Bambi’s friend, the rabbit “Thumper”, was an invention, as was “Flower” the skunk. Cute though they are, Felix Salten’s countryside is solidly European, with not a skunk or woodchuck in sight.<br /><br /><i>“In early summer the trees stood still under the blue sky, held their limbs outstretched and received the direct rays of the sun. On the shrubs and bushes in the undergrowth, the flowers unfolded their red, white and yellow stars. On some the seed pods had begin to appear again. They perched innumerable on the fine tips of the branches, tender and firm and resolute, and seemed like small clenched fists. Out of the earth came whole troops of flowers like motley stars, so that the soil of the twilit forest floor shone with a silent, ardent, colourful gladness. Everything smelled of fresh leaves, of blossom, of moist clods and green woods. When morning broke, or when the sun went down, the whole woods resounded with a thousand voices, and from morning till night the bees hummed, the wasps droned, and filled the fragrant stillness with their murmur.”</i><br /><br />Opening the book at random it would be easy to find such a passage. This extract is from the first chapter, and conveys the experience of a very young fawn, only days old. It perfectly conveys the sensations and impressions of early summer. Where I am at the moment, I can see clumps of yellow primroses, daisies, and a few grape hyacinths; over my shoulder is an abundance of cowslip flowers, their frilly yellow bells dancing among the blades of grass, and along the path, another mass of bluebells. If I walk a while, there will be great banks of primroses and celandines. The freshly budding trees: oaks, birches and sycamores are beginning to lighten the world with their light green reflections, some clusters of wood anemones nestling in the grass between, bluebells dotted around, and masses of white wild garlic flowers in a field nearby. This is my experience right now. The sensations I feel as I look at all the wild bounty surrounding me (which I have feebly attempted to describe), is akin to the feelings triggered by reading this book. The enchantment of Bambi’s world can be conjured up regardless of where we read it. There is no need to be far from the city.<br /><br />The story of Bambi is set in a forest, whose location is never specified. It was inspired by woods which Felix Salten discovered, when he was on holiday in the Alps. Bambi’s woodland home feels remote enough from humans to be a haven for wild creatures—but only for some of the time. There is a darkness in the book, which is at the heart of the story, and its important message. They animals are never completely free of the fear of <i>“He”</i>. a strange and incomprehensible being who invades the peace of the forest. <i>“He”</i> has the smell of death; an unearthly predator who possesses a “third arm”, which destroys and kills seemingly irregardless, and at a remote distance.<br /><br />Many children’s books about animals portray the natural world as benign and kindly, but the forest here is no such place. Death, even violent death, is accepted as part of the natural scheme of things. There is great friendship in the forest, but there are many natural enemies too. Perhaps Felix Salten’s negative view of, in particular, human’s relationship with the natural world is not so surprising. <b>Bambi</b> was written in 1923, in the aftermath of the First World War, and many Europeans at that time, having witnessed the inhumanity of war, turned towards the idea of a natural world as a haven. What distinguishes this book is that although we see and yearn for Bambi’s world, we also see that nature is indeed red in tooth and claw, and never more so than when <i>“He”</i> enters the scene.<br /><br />Felix Salten’s experiences after writing <b>Bambi</b> must only have confirmed his views of man’s inhumanity. He was Jewish, and life in Austria became increasingly perilous for a prominent Jew during the 1930s. In 1936, Adolf Hitler had Felix Salten’s books banned. Two years later, after Germany’s annexation of Austria, Felix Salten and his wife fled to Zurich, Switzerland, and lived there for the rest of their lives.<br /><br />Essentially <b>Bambi: A life in the Woods</b> is a coming of age story, in which we also learn much about the different wild creatures’ habits. It is anthropomorphic, but completely unsentimental. The writer John Galsworthy, who wrote the introduction, says:<br /><br /><i>“Bambi is a delicious book … Felix Salten is a poet … I do not, as a rule, like the method which places human words in the mouths of dumb creatures, and it is the triumph of this book that, behind the conversation, one feels the real sensations of the creatures who speak. Clear and illuminating, and in places very moving, it is a little masterpiece.”</i><br /><br />As well as this interesting short introduction, my copy has line drawings, plus a few lovely delicate water-colour plates, by Sylvia Green.<br /><br /><b>Bambi: A life in the Woods</b> begins when Bambi is born, in a thicket in the woods. He is an awkward and innocent young fawn, and his mother has to teach him what it is to be a deer: both the joys and the fears. Anyone who has ever watched days-old lambs gambolling in the fields, will recognise this:<br /><br /><i>“Now he saw the whole heaven stretching far and wide and he rejoiced without knowing why. In the forest he had only seen a stray sunbeam now and then, or the tender, dappled light that played through the branches. Suddenly he was standing in the blinding hot sunlight whose boundless power was beaming upon him. He stood in the splendid warmth that made him shut his eyes but opened his heart.<br /><br />Bambi was as though bewitched. He was completely beside himself with pleasure. He was simply wild. He leaped into the air, three, four, five times. He had to do it. He felt a terrible desire to leap and jump. He stretched his young limbs joyfully. His breath came deeply and easily. He drank in the air. The sweet smell of the meadow made him so wildly happy that he had to leap into the air.“</i><br /><br />Felix Salten seems to gets right inside how it feels to be a particular animal. He describes how Bambi learns that deer do not kill other animals or fight over food. Bambi learns too, that for deer it is only safe to go to the meadow early in the morning and late in the evening, and that signals such as the rustle of last year’s dead leaves will warn them of approaching danger. Bambi learns to fear storms, and to enjoy more gentle rain. He begins to encounter other animals in the meadow, such as a grasshopper, a butterfly, and a hare. But he is most excited when he meets his cousins with their mother, Ena. They are the lovely doe Faline, and the foolish, delicate young buck, Gobo. And he is a little in awe, when two impressive stags with spreading antlers on their heads come crashing out of the forest. Bambi’s mother tells him quietly that one of these proud and grand stags is his father.<br /><br />As he grows older, Bambi learns more about the sounds and smells of the forest. He is confused that sometimes his mother goes off by herself, and he goes calling, in search of her. But as he stands at the edge of a clearing:<br /><br /><i>“[Bambi] suddenly felt as if he were rooted to the ground and could not move.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[On the edge of the clearing, by a tall hazel bush, a creature was standing. Bambi had never seen such a creature before. At the same time the air brought him such a scent as he had never smelled in his life. It was a strange smell, heavy and acrid. It excited him to the point of madness … A kind of dread emanated from that face, a cold terror. That face had a tremendous power over him. It was tremendously painful to look at that face, and yet Bambi stood starting fixedly at it.“ <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></i><br /><br />Bambi is terrified, and runs for cover, his mother appearing suddenly to run by his side. When they are both safe in their glade once more, his mother tells him, <i>“That was “He”.”</i> <br /><br />Bambi shudders. He is learning to understand what it is to be a deer. Yet he still does not like to be alone, and frequently calls for his mother. On one occasion a magnificent stag stands before him, and asks him what he is crying about, <i>“Can’t you stay by yourself? Shame on you!”</i> he scolds.<br /><br />Then he is gone, leaving the little fawn crestfallen. Bambi almost understands his true nature, and how he is expected to behave as a roe deer, but he has not attained full maturity. He is learning all the time, through his experience, his strong inner sensations and instincts, and the teaching he gets.<br /><br />Bambi meets others in the woods, including <i>“Old Prince”</i>, the most magnificent, and wisest stag in the forest, whom he will meet again, when he has cause to be proud of himself. And he witnesses a great tragedy in the great meadow, which shocks him to the core. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ One morning he and his mother are grazing, when one of the stags comes out of the forest. Suddenly, there is a great booming blast of fire. The huge stag leaps into the air and then falls dead. Bambi senses with increasingly certainty that somehow <i>“He”</i> is the cause of it. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Summer gradually merges into Autumn and then becomes winter. Snow falls, and fresh grass is no longer easy to find. All the deer become more friendly during the cold months, and we see Bambi meeting with others; new faces such as <i>“Marena”</i>, and <i>“Netla”</i>, as well as his cousins. Bambi develops a great admiration for the fully grown stags, and longs to be like them, especially admiring <i>“Ronno”</i>, a fully grown stag who escaped after a hunter wounded him in the foot. Yet they all talk and worry about <i>“He”</i>, whom they know to be such a threat, and do not understand, and they tell each other terrifying stories about the third hand from which flame comes.<br /><br />There is another tragedy, involving <i>“He”</i> and many others like him. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Bambi was never to see his mother again after this. Bambi manages to run away because he hears a voice telling him to, but many of the animals do not survive the onslaught.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>This a very upsetting and graphic part of the book.<br /><br />During the harsh winter months Bambi learns that not all the animals are so friendly any more, and those who used to merely tolerate each other no longer do so. Food is in short supply, and different species, starving and cold, start to prey on their natural enemies. But eventually the season passes, and by now Bambi has grown, and developed little budding antlers. Spring also brings new delicious feelings which unsettle him. The doe Faline seems to him to be getting more and more beautiful, but she is attended by two adult stags, the important stag who had been wounded, Ronno, and another fully grown stag, <i>“Karus”</i> . They no longer want Bambi to come near Faline.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ There is a serious fight between Bambi and Ronno, which Bambi wins. Karus flees the scene, and Faline is happy to become his mate. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Another crucial time for Bambi’s coming of age happens is when <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Bambi wakes up and can hear Faline calling him. He is desperate to be with her, but is prevented from doing so by an older deer, who tells Bambi that <i>“He”</i> often uses tricks such as sounding like the calling of a deer, to entice them into danger. Bambi asks Faline never to call him again, because he would never be able to resist her call. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />One of the bucks who had disappeared earlier, returns to the story, and we learn his tale. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Gobo, Faline’s brother, had almost been killed in the night of the mass slaughter, but had been taken home by <i>“He”</i>, and taken care of. Now he seems fit and well and tells everyone how good <i>“He”</i> is, and that there is nothing to fear. Gobo wears a ribbon round his neck with pride, and openly grazes in the field at all times of day. But an older and wiser stag pities him, and we know that Gobo is destined for a terrible fate. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />This is one of many parts of the story which conjures up the later masterpiece, <i>“Watership Down”</i> to me. In that there is a character who is what we would call psychic, and has premonitions, and in <b>Bambi</b> we see aspects of the animals’ sensory perceptions which we humans cannot perceive. We also see individuals who are taken in, trapped and deceived by humans for their own ends. We see how humans routinely use the talents of animals for their own purposes, taking no account of the pain and devastation this may cause. There is much in common between some of the characters and the subtler elements of the story lines, and the ultimate message of these two books is the same.<br /><br />There is much anguish towards the end of the story, but we see great courage, a determination to survive, and loyal friendship. We see how Bambi learns that <i>“He”</i> is simply a part of the world, as Bambi is himself, and that that they are both a part of something much bigger. There is a mystical, other-worldly suggestion, as Bambi learns the cycle of life, and an older stag, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ his father, “Old Prince”, who had cared for him when he was also wounded by a hunter <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> says that it is time for him to go to the place where everyone goes alone. <br /><br />It is not yet Bambi’s time. He has matured into a fine strong stag, and when he spots a couple of confused young fawns, oddly familiar in appearance, he find himself reiterating the words the stag once said to him, when he was a young buck, <i>“Can’t you stay by yourself?”</i><br /><br />Bambi’s world has come full circle.<br /><br />This is a magical story, and one which teaches many lessons, to do with friendship, kindness and courage. No matter how difficult life may become, the author insists, we must never give up. We must never take what appears to be an easy way out. We may be able to rely on our friends, to help us overcome those difficulties, and must stay true to what we know is good. If we keep trying, in the end everything will resolve. Just like Bambi, the fawn who became a strong and proud stag, we will also stay true to our nature, and emerge stronger.<br /><br />But of course the main message of the story is a warning to humanity. A dominant species such as humanity carries great responsibility. In <b>Bambi</b>, <i>“He”</i> is the creatures’ worst enemy: cold, distant and deadly to animals. Felix Salten believed that all animals—and Nature—deserve our respect. We have a duty to care for animals, and not kill them for our own desires, such as for sport, (or possibly, a sub-text here, is for food). When the Third Reich banned his books, it was not just because of Felix Salten’s ethnicity, but also because they found its anti-hunting message offensive to their ideology.<br /><br />Felix Salten’s views ring loud and clear in this book, and they are timeless. Even the most minor characters are shown to reveal truths. Nettla is an old doe, who is self-sufficient and wise, with her own ideas about everything. She regards <i>“He”</i> with disgust, and can be seen every day in elderly people who are sceptical about “progress” for the wrong reasons, feeling that they have seen this all before. Marena reveals the other side of the coin. She is a young, half-grown doe who predicts that <i>“He”</i> will some day be as gentle as the deer themselves. Young and innocent, she is eternally optimistic.<br /><br />Yet I cannot help dreaming a little, and hoping that Marena’s imagined future will come true.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1998947785675594894" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_134824701" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/134824701" />
    <a title="Charity" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/424546-charity"><img alt="Charity" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496973255p2/424546.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/134824701?book_show_action=true">Dec 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charity" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charity" href="/user/show/424546-charity">Charity</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/424546-charity?shelf=kids">kids</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer134824701" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3956421037875116641">This is not Disney. This book was incredible. The description was so real, the language not dumbed down for kids. Yes, the animals talked, but it wasn't cutesy, silly talking. It was <i>Watership Down</i> kind of talking. I read this to my five-and-a-half-year-old daughter (I'm trying to give her the original versions of all of the stories before she sees the Disney versions). When the first scary thing happened, I worried that I'd made a horrible mistake. But, although she was upset, she put it all in</span>
  <span id="freeText3956421037875116641" style="display:none">This is not Disney. This book was incredible. The description was so real, the language not dumbed down for kids. Yes, the animals talked, but it wasn't cutesy, silly talking. It was <i>Watership Down</i> kind of talking. I read this to my five-and-a-half-year-old daughter (I'm trying to give her the original versions of all of the stories before she sees the Disney versions). When the first scary thing happened, I worried that I'd made a horrible mistake. But, although she was upset, she put it all into perspective very quickly. I loved watching her build a connection with the characters. At one point she said something like, "The words seem so real!" How wonderful to watch my daughter's love of reading and storytelling develop!<br /><br /><i>Update</i>: I've thought about this book overnight and now I've got a few more things I wanted to add. Like another reviewer mentioned, the conversation between two leaves at the end of fall was masterful. There are so many issues of life and death and of God and of domestication. I was thinking about this book in the context of Salten's life as a Jew in Austria before the second World War. Apparently, he was born in Budapest in 1869 and moved to Vienna when he was just a baby because in 1867, Jews got full citizenship in Vienna. This book was published in 1926, and I'm just starting to think about the messages in the book in the context of what was going on in Europe at the time. There are just so many layers of understanding. No wonder I found this book so satisfying.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3956421037875116641" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Hilary and Mischenko</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15194189-leila?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15194189-leila?shelf=my-challenge-books">my-challenge-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15194189-leila?shelf=so-many-books-so-little-time">so-many-books-so-little-time</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15194189-leila?shelf=children-s-books">children-s-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15194189-leila?shelf=animal-stories">animal-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15194189-leila?shelf=my-wow-books">my-wow-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15194189-leila?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15194189-leila?shelf=children-to-young-adult">children-to-young-adult</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2273259217" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2650856688283404500">What a jewel this little book is! I did read it very many years ago but had forgotten just how wonderful and sensitive Felix Saltern's writing is. I have watched the delightful and popular movie many times... with my children and then with my grandchildren but I had forgotten how different the book is, There is no Thumper or Flower for example. Felix Saltern clearly feels deeply for nature and animals. Although in the book the animals "talk" which is not to everyone's taste, the story manages so</span>
  <span id="freeText2650856688283404500" style="display:none">What a jewel this little book is! I did read it very many years ago but had forgotten just how wonderful and sensitive Felix Saltern's writing is. I have watched the delightful and popular movie many times... with my children and then with my grandchildren but I had forgotten how different the book is, There is no Thumper or Flower for example. Felix Saltern clearly feels deeply for nature and animals. Although in the book the animals "talk" which is not to everyone's taste, the story manages somehow to capture the inner feelings of the creatures.<br /><br />It is in many ways such a gentle book, full of wisdom, yet there is violence too. Even this is handled in a sensible way. Bambi of course is the main character and through him the ifs and buts of growing up is explored. He is nurtured by his Mother. She protects him and teaches him about the great dangers that the deer must face. His Father, the great Prince of the forest takes over his care later in the book. He experiences love, joy and fun as a youngster but also has to face tragedy too. He learns about the seasons and how to survive and grows through problems he initially doesn't understand and finds hard to overcome. The greatest danger to the creatures is "He" in other words "Man"<br /><br />This book can be read by all ages but older children will be able to read it for themselves. It is also a good book to read to younger children too but there are other editions for the much younger children with lots of pictures which would be perhaps a better choice than this particular edition. It is a book about wisdom, love, courage, discernment whilst learning the lessons of life. I found it to be a delightful experience to read this book again after so many years,</span>
  <a data-text-id="2650856688283404500" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_897517460" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/897517460" />
    <a title="booklady" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/350218-booklady"><img alt="booklady" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591723471p2/350218.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/897517460?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="booklady" class="user" itemprop="url" name="booklady" href="/user/show/350218-booklady">booklady</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/6238604-bambi">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=all-ages">all-ages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=children">children</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=nature">nature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer897517460" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9204344650853227129">The book, <i>Bambi</i>, is slightly different than its Disney counterpart. I know, big surprise. There are still many similarities and in general the two versions track. The book begins with the birth of Bambi and his all-important first relationship with his gentle, loving mother, his childhood friendship with Faline and his early learning about ‘Him’ (who is never named) but we know to be man or men, specifically hunters with their third arm. In time Bambi becomes aware of the old stag who continues </span>
  <span id="freeText9204344650853227129" style="display:none">The book, <i>Bambi</i>, is slightly different than its Disney counterpart. I know, big surprise. There are still many similarities and in general the two versions track. The book begins with the birth of Bambi and his all-important first relationship with his gentle, loving mother, his childhood friendship with Faline and his early learning about ‘Him’ (who is never named) but we know to be man or men, specifically hunters with their third arm. In time Bambi becomes aware of the old stag who continues to appear during critical moments in the young buck’s life. One day the true identity of this pivotal mentor is revealed to Bambi. <br /><br />My description below gives away part of the ending, so if you want to be surprised, don't read on. But <i>do</i> read the book! I need to re-watch the movie to see if this scene is included and how it was handled. <br /><br /><b>Spoiler Alert!</b> One day upon hearing three shots fired in the woods, the old stag, Bambi’s father, leads his son to the scene of the shooting, the very <i>last</i> place Bambi wants to go. There at his feet Bambi sees ‘Him’, or as he now knows, just an ordinary man, the victim of a shooting. The dialogue between the two deer is illustrative of a revelation and is as follows:<br /><br />‘“Do you see Bambi,” the old stag went on, “do you see how He’s lying there dead like one of us? Listen Bambi. He isn’t all-powerful as they say. Everything that lives and grows doesn’t come from Him. He isn’t above us. He’s just the same as we are. He has the same fears, the same needs, and suffers in the same way. He can be killed like us, and then he lies helpless on the ground like all the rest of us, as you see Him now.” <br /><br />There was a silence.<br /><br />“Do you understand me Bambi?” asked the old stag.<br /><br />“I think so.” Bambi said in a whisper.<br /><br />“Then speak,” the old stag commanded. <br /> <br />Bambi was inspired, and said trembling, “There is Another who is over us all, over us and over Him.”<br /><br />“Now I can go,” said the old stag.’<br /><br />Bambi is a classic which I pray will never go out of style or print, not because I’m necessarily advocating one way or another on the issues of guns or hunting, but because it’s a story with the important reminder that there is One greater than all of us—men and beasts—and He cares very much for <i>all</i> His creatures, great and small.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9204344650853227129" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3308384943" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3308384943?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ken" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ken" href="/user/show/38999260-ken">Ken</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3308384943" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7606563947915601770">When the name 'Bambi' is mentioned, everyone instantly thinks of the 1942 Disney movie.<br/>The loosely adapted animation is actually based on this 1923 novel by the Austrian author Felix Salten.<br/><br/>Whilst the film hits the same story beats, the two couldn't feel more different.<br/>This is a dark harsher experience for the young dear.<br/><br/>The differences are plain to see from the outset as Bambi is born with only a single magpie as a witness, the movie includes a plethora of animals.<br/>There's no Thumper or Flower e</span>
  <span id="freeText7606563947915601770" style="display:none">When the name 'Bambi' is mentioned, everyone instantly thinks of the 1942 Disney movie.<br />The loosely adapted animation is actually based on this 1923 novel by the Austrian author Felix Salten.<br /><br />Whilst the film hits the same story beats, the two couldn't feel more different.<br />This is a dark harsher experience for the young dear.<br /><br />The differences are plain to see from the outset as Bambi is born with only a single magpie as a witness, the movie includes a plethora of animals.<br />There's no Thumper or Flower either.<br /><br />While the original novel doesn't have to cuteness of the Disney movie, it doesn't shy away from the horrors that animals face from hunters.<br />The way that humans are described as 'Him' is very effective.<br /><br />Don't expect this to appeal to younger children, the themes will appeal more to a pre-teen reader.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7606563947915601770" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2999387744" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/66567613-luisa-knight?shelf=family-read-aloud">family-read-aloud</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/66567613-luisa-knight?shelf=childrens-classics">childrens-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/66567613-luisa-knight?shelf=children">children</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2999387744" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17461280707237716776">What a beautifully written masterpiece! I was completely surprised and overwhelmed by the gorgeous scenes of forest and woodland animals that so nimbly sprung to life from within these pages. Autumn, Winter, Spring, each passing of the seasons with their unique enchantment and starkness, were so vividly expressed. The animal's joys and heartaches told with such feeling it was hard to stay removed from the story. It is a noteworthy classic and one I heartily recommend!<br/><br/>The author, a nature-lover a</span>
  <span id="freeText17461280707237716776" style="display:none">What a beautifully written masterpiece! I was completely surprised and overwhelmed by the gorgeous scenes of forest and woodland animals that so nimbly sprung to life from within these pages. Autumn, Winter, Spring, each passing of the seasons with their unique enchantment and starkness, were so vividly expressed. The animal's joys and heartaches told with such feeling it was hard to stay removed from the story. It is a noteworthy classic and one I heartily recommend!<br /><br />The author, a nature-lover and a sportsman, kept the story well balanced. While the animals are personified, they do not deviate from their natural tendencies; much to my chagrin at times. I had wanted Bambi and Faline to stay together always, but as male deer do, he wandered off now and again after the mating season. This, when you're not carried away in the climax of a story point, is the better way to go, as the book offers it's readers a more educational look at nature and not merely a story.<br /><br />If you're a hunter, hiker, animal lover, appreciator of exemplary writing or just looking for your next family read aloud, this book is for you. The animated cartoon deviates enough to provide some nice surprises too.<br /><br />Ages: 8+<br /><br />Cleanliness: Like the Disney cartoon, there are some tense moments when the hunters are out and animals are being shot. A few scenes are described using a sentence or two about blood flowing from the neck etc. <br /><br />**Like my reviews? I also have hundreds of detailed reports that I offer too. These reports give a complete break-down of everything in the book, so you'll know just how clean it is or isn't. I also have Clean Guides (downloadable PDFs) which enable you to clean up your book before reading it! <br /><br /><a href="https://thebookradar.com/" target="_blank;" rel="nofollow noopener">Visit my website!</a></span>

<div id="review_150694823" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150694823" />
    <a title="Manybooks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2970379-manybooks"><img alt="Manybooks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459773375p2/2970379.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150694823?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manybooks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manybooks" href="/user/show/2970379-manybooks">Manybooks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/30309948-bambi">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=bildungsroman">bildungsroman</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=childrens-literature">childrens-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=translations">translations</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=book-reviews">book-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=animal-tale">animal-tale</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150694823" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8483502953688841576">So after at first really and truly needing to get a bit used to Felix Salten's general writing style (and also having to lose my annoyance that in the beginning of <u>Bambi: Eine Lebensgeschichte aus dem Walde</u>, when Felix Salten is describing Bambi as a newly-born fawn, he makes use of the words <i>blöd</i> and <i>duselig</i>, he writes two descriptive adjectives that basically in German usually tend to signify major stupidity and are also often specifically used as insults in order to describe young Bambi as st</span>
  <span id="freeText8483502953688841576" style="display:none">So after at first really and truly needing to get a bit used to Felix Salten's general writing style (and also having to lose my annoyance that in the beginning of <u>Bambi: Eine Lebensgeschichte aus dem Walde</u>, when Felix Salten is describing Bambi as a newly-born fawn, he makes use of the words <i>blöd</i> and <i>duselig</i>, he writes two descriptive adjectives that basically in German usually tend to signify major stupidity and are also often specifically used as insults in order to describe young Bambi as still being rather unresponsive and unsteady on his feet during his first hours of life), I did in fact and indeed end up for the most part rather enjoying <u>Bambi: Eine Lebensgeschichte aus dem Walde</u> and in particular appreciating how Bambi in classic bildungsroman fashion develops and matures from a helpless fawn utterly dependent on others (actually mostly on his mother) into a strong and courageous stag not only able to adequately take care of himself but to also provide protection and succour to the other deer of the forest (experiencing both joy and tragedy, both heartbreak and triumph, living both the positives and equally all of the negatives of existence). <br /><br />However, that having been said and while I have certainly found <u>Bambi: Eine Lebensgeschichte aus dem Walde</u> an engaging and also often enlightening reading experience, I also cannot say that this novel, that <u>Bambi: Eine Lebensgeschichte aus dem Walde</u> has been an unmitigated and total reading pleasure for me. For personally and emotionally, I do tend to think that the rather constant threat to Bambi and to the forest as a whole posed by man (as mostly a hunter) while perhaps realistic to a point also feels within the storyline of <u>Bambi: Eine Lebensgeschichte aus dem Walde</u> and Felix Salten's narrative as sometimes being more than a trifle exaggerated and overly extreme (and as such also often as though Felix Salten is literally wallowing joyfully in describing one hunter-based horror and terror after another, that he is really overdoing things and almost wanting to frighten his readers and to make man just into one huge threat to and for nature, and just man as a HE at that, as an anonymous male entity, almost as though those of us who are of the female gender somehow never would choose to hunt and therefore cannot be an equal danger to nature, to the forest and to its denizens).<br /><br />Combined with the fact that although I do generally have no textual issues with the animals in <u>Bambi: Eine Lebensgeschichte aus dem Walde</u> freely talking and conversing amongst themselves (and that thankfully, they only talk amongst themselves and do not talk to humans), I certainly and actually would much prefer it if Felix Salten ONLY had Bambi and the rest of the forest deer chatting amongst themselves instead of having Bambi et al also converse with birds, insects etc. (and yes, I really do not like that scene in <u>Bambi: Eine Lebensgeschichte aus dem Walde</u> where two leaves are having a conversation). And therefore, while I am most definitely very glad to have finally read <u>Bambi: Eine Lebensgeschichte aus dem Walde</u> (in its original German text and unabridged), I also cannot and will not consider it a personal favourite (as to and for me, albeit that <u>Bambi: Eine Lebensgeschichte aus dem Walde</u> is a good enough story and an engaging enough story in an of itself, it is also a tale that I for one do wish were a bit less focused on hunting and with a narration that just had one animal species and not multiple ones chatting with one another).</span>
  <a data-text-id="8483502953688841576" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13340175600066753412" style="display:none">"'He was very nice to me. And I like him so much. He's so wonderful and green..."<br /><br />"'Can it be true,' said the first leaf, 'can it really be true, that others come to take our places when we're gone and after them still others, and more and more?'<br />'It is really true,' whispered the second leaf. 'We can't even begin to imagine it, it's beyond our powers.'<br />'It makes me very sad,' added the first lead.<br />They were silent for a while. Then the first leaf said quietly to herself, 'Why must we fall?'...<br />The second lead asked, 'What happens to us when we have fallen?'<br />'We sink down...'<br />'What is under us?'<br />The first leaf answers, 'I don't know, some say one thing, some another, but nobody knows.'<br />The second lead asked, 'Do we feel anything, do we know anything about ourselves when we're down there?'<br />The first leaf answered, 'Who knows? Not one of all those down there has ever come back to tell us about it.'<br />They were silent again. Then the first leaf said tenderly to the other, 'Don't worry so much about it, you're trembling.'<br />'That's nothing,' the second leaf answered, 'I tremble at the least thing now. I don't feel so sure of my hold as I used to.'<br />(........)<br />She was silent, but went on after a little while, 'Which of us will go first?'<br />'There's still plenty of time to worry about that,' the other lead assured her. 'Let's remember how beautiful it was, how wonderful, when the sun came out and shone so warmly that we thought we'd burst with life. Do you remember? And the morning dew, and the mild and splendid nights....'<br />(........)<br />'No, really,' the first lead exclaimed eagerly, 'believe me, you're as lovely as the day you were born. Here and there may be a little yellow spot but it's hardly noticeable and only makes you handsomer, believe me.'<br />'Thanks,' whispered the second leaf, quite touched. 'I don't believe you, not altogether, but I thank you because you're so kind, you've always been so kind to me. I'm just beginning to understand how kind you are.'"</span>

<div id="review_282965002" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/282965002" />
    <a title="Stefan Yates" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7590340-stefan-yates"><img alt="Stefan Yates" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1328116275p2/7590340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/282965002?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stefan Yates" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stefan Yates" href="/user/show/7590340-stefan-yates">Stefan Yates</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7590340-stefan-yates?shelf=kids-room">kids-room</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7590340-stefan-yates?shelf=stefan-dusty-bookshelf-2014">stefan-dusty-bookshelf-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer282965002" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13147792586515237082">I'm really not completely sure how I feel about this book now that I've finished it. On one hand, it is well written and there are scenes that illustrate the beauty and innocence of nature so vividly that one is able to picture them even without the wonderful illustrations of this particular version. Yet, there is also much of this book that deals with the violent and grisly destruction that humans bring to the wildlife of the forest.<br/><br/>How this became regarded as a children's story, I'm not really</span>
  <span id="freeText13147792586515237082" style="display:none">I'm really not completely sure how I feel about this book now that I've finished it. On one hand, it is well written and there are scenes that illustrate the beauty and innocence of nature so vividly that one is able to picture them even without the wonderful illustrations of this particular version. Yet, there is also much of this book that deals with the violent and grisly destruction that humans bring to the wildlife of the forest.<br /><br />How this became regarded as a children's story, I'm not really sure. The scenes of death throughout the book are graphic and horrifying and there is a true feeling of absolute terror at times that is truly palpable. To me, this book is being aimed directly at those who choose to kill and destroy for the pure thrill and feeling of power that they derive from it and is an attempt to illustrate the destruction that these kind of actions provoke. It's done through very powerful imagery and makes one very uncomfortable at times.<br /><br />Why Disney decided to make this into a mostly feel-good story, I'm not sure, but those unsuspecting children who decided to read the book that one of their favorite films was based upon were surely exposed to quite a shocking experience.<br /><br />I didn't hate it, but I can't really say that I enjoyed it all that much either.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13147792586515237082" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_110856310" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/110856310" />
    <a title="Shanna Gonzalez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1533441-shanna-gonzalez"><img alt="Shanna Gonzalez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1221561813p2/1533441.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/110856310?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shanna Gonzalez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shanna Gonzalez" href="/user/show/1533441-shanna-gonzalez">Shanna Gonzalez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1533441-shanna-gonzalez?shelf=children-12">children-12</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer110856310" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15651187260361416605">Most of my generation, when they think of Bambi, think of the sentimental Disney movie. But the original book was a serious work. Its one-of-a-kind conception and spectacular writing have earned it classic status as the story of a young deer growing to adulthood in his woodland home. Salten's writing is compelling, and scenes from the story will stay in the mind long after reading, to be often recalled and savored. This Bambi lives a grim and dangerous life, his world filled with blood and fear </span>
  <span id="freeText15651187260361416605" style="display:none">Most of my generation, when they think of Bambi, think of the sentimental Disney movie. But the original book was a serious work. Its one-of-a-kind conception and spectacular writing have earned it classic status as the story of a young deer growing to adulthood in his woodland home. Salten's writing is compelling, and scenes from the story will stay in the mind long after reading, to be often recalled and savored. This Bambi lives a grim and dangerous life, his world filled with blood and fear -- and most of his troubles emanate from the dreaded Man.<br /><br />Bambi's response to his pitiless world is to emulate his distant father, exiling himself from the company of others -- even Faline, his mate. In abandoning these relationships, he chooses a solitary life in which he accepts loneliness (his own and Faline's) as the price of his safety.<br /><br />There is a clear sense that Bambi's coming-of-age is defined by no longer needing to rely on others. His relationship with his father begins when the old stag rebukes him for calling his mother: "Your mother has no time for you. Can't you stay by yourself?" Later in life, his father befriends him and teaches him to move through the forest so that he is never seen. The relationship ends when his father leaves him to die alone. Bambi then passes on the heritage of solitude by rebuking two fawns for crying after their mother.<br /><br />In contrast to this message of isolationism, the Bible teaches that a solitary existence falls short of God's plan (Genesis 2:18). Rather than affirming interdependence as a sign of weakness, Scripture teaches that people are meant to live in community (Ecclesiastes 4:12; 1 Corinthians 12:12; and Hebrews 10:24-25). Rather than becoming stronger when we withdraw from others, being alone actually makes us more vulnerable to attack.<br /><br />The romantic appeal of Bambi's self-chosen exile has an especial attraction in our individualistic culture. Adults will need to use discernment about when a child is ready for this book. The reader ought to be mature enough to critically engage Salten's perspective without being drawn into his isolationist impulse. Since the message is embedded in a masterfully written story, parents should be cautious about introducing it too early, but at the right time it ought to provide excellent material for discussion.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15651187260361416605" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1157749791" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1157749791" />
    <a title="Melissa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48646-melissa"><img alt="Melissa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495037182p2/48646.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1157749791?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa" href="/user/show/48646-melissa">Melissa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48646-melissa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48646-melissa?shelf=you-should-read-these">you-should-read-these</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48646-melissa?shelf=non-human-animals">non-human-animals</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1157749791" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15848734718378627368">I loved this book even more than I loved <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/76620.Watership_Down' title='Watership Down by Richard Adams' rel='nofollow noopener'>Watership Down</a>, and I loved Watership Down a lot. In fact, if anyone out there could tell me some more books that realistically portrays the thoughts of non-human animals, without personifying them in any way besides giving them verbal language, I'd appreciate the reading suggestion!<br/><br/>Bambi is a delightful book right from the start. I spend a lot of time with young children, and the way Bambi was so curious and innocent and annoying to his mother when he wa</span>
  <span id="freeText15848734718378627368" style="display:none">I loved this book even more than I loved <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/76620.Watership_Down" title="Watership Down by Richard Adams" rel="nofollow noopener">Watership Down</a>, and I loved Watership Down a lot. In fact, if anyone out there could tell me some more books that realistically portrays the thoughts of non-human animals, without personifying them in any way besides giving them verbal language, I'd appreciate the reading suggestion!<br /><br />Bambi is a delightful book right from the start. I spend a lot of time with young children, and the way Bambi was so curious and innocent and annoying to his mother when he was little was incredibly familiar and identifiable. I loved how the magpie mocked Bambi's momma only moments after she'd given birth to her baby, not understanding, as birds likely wouldn't, that live birth is exhausting. These things happen right at the beginning, but the book never fails to feel incredibly true to life all throughout.<br /><br />Humans, which the animals just call "He" or "Him," are horrific to the woodland creatures. I was already against hunting (as well as the eating of other animals by humans at all), but this book should definitely be read by anyone who claims that hunting is not cruel. I don't believe in any gods, but when I read a book like this, it's clear to me that humans are doing something hideous by thinking we are god-like, and can affect the lives of all other animals however we please. Even the way we've domesticated dogs is hideous. We made an enormous mistake by removing ourselves from nature. It keeps me up at night.<br /><br />All in all, Bambi is a serious book. It's sad, it'll make you think about our place in the natural world, and it's also beautiful and delightful and poetic. I loved it. I adored it. I think you should read it!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15848734718378627368" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5139117629163032252" style="display:none">Hmmm. I bought this book to read to my children because I thought it would be a richer, better story than the Disney movie I grew up with. (my kids have never seen it)<br />The writing is lovely, if heavy on adverbs, and there are some gorgeous descriptions of life in the forest and some profound observations on the harsh realities of the natural world. <br />But this is not a children's book. My three-year-old asked repeatedly that I NOT read it. My six-year-old was left with a blank, dissatisfied stare after the close of the final chapter. I believe I know why.<br />Salten had a perfect opportunity to weave some kind of hope or redemption into the tale. At the very end, Bambi, after having suffered and undergone many changes and gained the wisdom of the forest, meets his offspring. He's placed into the exact situation in which he found himself as a young fawn, in the moment when the wise old stag treated him harshly. Salten could have stopped here, had Bambi remember his own terror as a young fawn; he could have allowed Bambi to show some tenderness, even some fondness for what was lost. He chose not to. Instead, Bambi behaves in the same cold, dismissive manner in which he had been treated, and the reader is left with a feeling of hopelessness. <br />This is the fallen world. This is violence and winter and the bite of the hunter's bullet. The creatures murder one another and die in their turn. And all of it comes to nothing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5139117629163032252" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
